href="/w/index.php?title=Gini_coefficient&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Countries by Gini Index">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2014_Gini_Index_World_Map,_income_inequality_distribution_by_country_per_World_Bank.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/2014_Gini_Index_World_Map%2C_income_inequality_distribution_by_country_per_World_Bank.svg/400px-2014_Gini_Index_World_Map%2C_income_inequality_distribution_by_country_per_World_Bank.svg.png" width="400" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/2014_Gini_Index_World_Map%2C_income_inequality_distribution_by_country_per_World_Bank.svg/600px-2014_Gini_Index_World_Map%2C_income_inequality_distribution_by_country_per_World_Bank.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/2014_Gini_Index_World_Map%2C_income_inequality_distribution_by_country_per_World_Bank.svg/800px-2014_Gini_Index_World_Map%2C_income_inequality_distribution_by_country_per_World_Bank.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="863" data-file-height="443" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2014_Gini_Index_World_Map,_income_inequality_distribution_by_country_per_World_Bank.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries\' income inequality according to their most recent reported Gini index values (often 10+ years old) as of 2014:<br />red = high, green = low inequality</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_income_equality" title="List of countries by income equality">List of countries by income equality</a></div>\n<p>A Gini index value above 50 is considered high; countries including Brazil, Colombia, South Africa, Botswana, and Honduras can be found in this category. A Gini index value of 30 or above is considered medium; countries including Vietnam, Mexico, Poland, The United States, Argentina, Russia and Uruguay can be found in this category. A Gini index value lower than 30 is considered low; countries including Austria, Germany, Denmark, Slovenia, Sweden and Ukraine can be found in this category.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Limitations_of_Gini_coefficient">Limitations of Gini coefficient</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gini_coefficient&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Limitations of Gini coefficient">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Gini coefficient is a relative measure. Its proper use and interpretation is controversial.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> It is possible for the Gini coefficient of a developing country to rise (due to increasing inequality of income) while the number of people in absolute poverty decreases.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> This is because the Gini coefficient measures relative, not absolute, wealth. Changing income inequality, measured by Gini coefficients, can be due to structural changes in a society such as growing population (baby booms, aging populations, increased divorce rates, <a href="/wiki/Extended_family" title="Extended family">extended family</a> households splitting into <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear families</a>, emigration, immigration) and income mobility.<sup id="cite_ref-Kwok10_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kwok10-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Gini coefficients are simple, and this simplicity can lead to oversights and can confuse the comparison of different populations; for example, while both Bangladesh (per capita income of $1,693) and the Netherlands (per capita income of $42,183) had an income Gini coefficient of 0.31 in 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-undp2010a_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-undp2010a-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> the quality of life, economic opportunity and absolute income in these countries are very different, i.e. countries may have identical Gini coefficients, but differ greatly in wealth. Basic necessities may be available to all in a developed economy, while in an undeveloped economy with the same Gini coefficient, basic necessities may be unavailable to most or unequally available, due to lower absolute wealth.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="float: left; margin-right:1em;">\n<caption>Table A. Different income distributions<br />with the same Gini Index<sup id="cite_ref-faogini_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faogini-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Household<br />Group</th>\n<th>Country A<br />Annual<br />Income ($)</th>\n<th>Country B<br />Annual<br />Income ($)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>20,000</td>\n<td>9,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2</td>\n<td>30,000</td>\n<td>40,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>3</td>\n<td>40,000</td>\n<td>48,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>4</td>\n<td>50,000</td>\n<td>48,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>5</td>\n<td>60,000</td>\n<td>55,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Total Income</td>\n<td>$200,000</td>\n<td>$200,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Country\'s Gini</td>\n<td><b>0.2</b></td>\n<td><b>0.2</b>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<dl><dt>Different income distributions with the same Gini coefficient</dt></dl>\n<p>Even when the total income of a population is the same, in certain situations two countries with different income distributions can have the same Gini index (e.g. cases when income Lorenz Curves cross).<sup id="cite_ref-faogini_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faogini-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Table A illustrates one such situation. Both countries have a Gini coefficient of 0.2, but the average income distributions for household groups are different. As another example, in a population where the lowest 50% of individuals have no income and the other 50% have equal income, the Gini coefficient is 0.5; whereas for another population where the lowest 75% of people have 25% of income and the top 25% have 75% of the income, the Gini index is also 0.5. Economies with similar incomes and Gini coefficients can have very different income distributions. Bell\xc3\xb9 and Liberati claim that to rank income inequality between two different populations based on their Gini indices is sometimes not possible, or misleading.<sup id="cite_ref-Maio2007_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maio2007-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Extreme wealth inequality, yet low income Gini coefficient</dt></dl>\n<p>A Gini index does not contain information about absolute national or personal incomes. Populations can have very low income Gini indices, yet simultaneously very high wealth Gini index. By measuring inequality in income, the Gini ignores the differential efficiency of use of household income. By ignoring wealth (except as it contributes to income) the Gini can create the appearance of inequality when the people compared are at different stages in their life. Wealthy countries such as Sweden can show a low Gini coefficient for disposable income of 0.31 thereby appearing equal, yet have very high Gini coefficient for wealth of 0.79 to 0.86 thereby suggesting an extremely unequal wealth distribution in its society.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> These factors are not assessed in income-based Gini.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; float: right; margin-left:1em;">\n<caption>Table B. Same income distributions<br />but different Gini Index\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Household<br />number</th>\n<th>Country A<br />Annual<br />Income ($)</th>\n<th>Household<br />combined<br />number</th>\n<th>Country A<br />combined<br />Annual<br />Income ($)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>20,000</td>\n<td rowspan="2" style="text-align: center;">1 &amp; 2</td>\n<td rowspan="2" style="text-align: center;">50,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2</td>\n<td>30,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>3</td>\n<td>40,000</td>\n<td rowspan="2" style="text-align: center;">3 &amp; 4</td>\n<td rowspan="2" style="text-align: center;">90,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>4</td>\n<td>50,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>5</td>\n<td>60,000</td>\n<td rowspan="2" style="text-align: center;">5 &amp; 6</td>\n<td rowspan="2" style="text-align: center;">130,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>6</td>\n<td>70,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>7</td>\n<td>80,000</td>\n<td rowspan="2" style="text-align: center;">7 &amp; 8</td>\n<td rowspan="2" style="text-align: center;">170,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>8</td>\n<td>90,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>9</td>\n<td>120,000</td>\n<td rowspan="2" style="text-align: center;">9 &amp; 10</td>\n<td rowspan="2" style="text-align: center;">270,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>10</td>\n<td>150,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Total Income</td>\n<td>$710,000</td>\n<td></td>\n<td>$710,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Country\'s Gini</td>\n<td><b>0.303</b></td>\n<td></td>\n<td><b>0.293</b>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<dl><dt>Small sample bias \xe2\x80\x93 sparsely populated regions more likely to have low Gini coefficient</dt></dl>\n<p>Gini index has a downward-bias for small populations.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Counties or states or countries with small populations and less diverse economies will tend to report small Gini coefficients. For economically diverse large population groups, a much higher coefficient is expected than for each of its regions. Taking world economy as one, and income distribution for all human beings, for example, different scholars estimate global Gini index to range between 0.61 and 0.68.<sup id="cite_ref-fao2009_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fao2009-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-undp10_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-undp10-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\nAs with other inequality coefficients, the Gini coefficient is influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Granularity" title="Granularity">granularity</a> of the measurements. For example, five 20% quantiles (low granularity) will usually yield a lower Gini coefficient than twenty 5% quantiles (high granularity) for the same distribution. Philippe Monfort has shown that using inconsistent or unspecified granularity limits the usefulness of Gini coefficient measurements.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Gini coefficient measure gives different results when applied to individuals instead of households, for the same economy and same income distributions. If household data is used, the measured value of income Gini depends on how the household is defined. When different populations are not measured with consistent definitions, comparison is not meaningful.\n</p><p>Deininger and Squire (1996) show that income Gini coefficient based on individual income, rather than household income, are different. For example, for the United States, they find that the individual income-based Gini index was 0.35, while for France it was 0.43. According to their individual focused method, in the 108 countries they studied, South Africa had the world\'s highest Gini coefficient at 0.62, Malaysia had Asia\'s highest Gini coefficient at 0.5, Brazil the highest at 0.57 in Latin America and Caribbean region, and Turkey the highest at 0.5 in OECD countries.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; float: left; margin-right:1em;">\n<caption>Table C. Household money income<br />distributions and Gini Index, US<sup id="cite_ref-uscb2011_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uscb2011-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Income bracket<br />(in 2010 adjusted dollars)</th>\n<th>% of Population<br />1979</th>\n<th>% of Population<br />2010\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Under $15,000</td>\n<td>14.6%</td>\n<td>13.7%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>$15,000 \xe2\x80\x93 $24,999</td>\n<td>11.9%</td>\n<td>12.0%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>$25,000 \xe2\x80\x93 $34,999</td>\n<td>12.1%</td>\n<td>10.9%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>$35,000 \xe2\x80\x93 $49,999</td>\n<td>15.4%</td>\n<td>13.9%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>$50,000 \xe2\x80\x93 $74,999</td>\n<td>22.1%</td>\n<td>17.7%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>$75,000 \xe2\x80\x93 $99,999</td>\n<td>12.4%</td>\n<td>11.4%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>$100,000 \xe2\x80\x93 $149,999</td>\n<td>8.3%</td>\n<td>12.1%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>$150,000 \xe2\x80\x93 $199,999</td>\n<td>2.0%</td>\n<td>4.5%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>$200,000 and over</td>\n<td>1.2%</td>\n<td>3.9%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Total Households</td>\n<td>80,776,000</td>\n<td>118,682,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>United States\' Gini<br />on pre-tax basis</td>\n<td><b>0.404</b></td>\n<td><b>0.469</b>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<dl><dt>Gini coefficient is unable to discern the effects of structural changes in populations<sup id="cite_ref-Kwok10_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kwok10-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup></dt></dl>\n<p>Expanding on the importance of life-span measures, the Gini coefficient as a point-estimate of equality at a certain time, ignores life-span changes in income. Typically, increases in the proportion of young or old members of a society will drive apparent changes in equality, simply because people generally have lower incomes and wealth when they are young than when they are old. Because of this, factors such as age distribution within a population and mobility within income classes can create the appearance of inequality when none exist taking into account demographic effects. Thus a given economy may have a higher Gini coefficient at any one point in time compared to another, while the Gini coefficient calculated over individuals\' lifetime income is actually lower than the apparently more equal (at a given point in time) economy\'s.<sup id="cite_ref-blomq81_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blomq81-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Essentially, what matters is not just inequality in any particular year, but the composition of the distribution over time.\n</p><p>Kwok claims income Gini coefficient for Hong Kong has been high (0.434 in 2010<sup id="cite_ref-undp2010a_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-undp2010a-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>), in part because of structural changes in its population. Over recent decades, Hong Kong has witnessed increasing numbers of small households, elderly households and elderly living alone. The combined income is now split into more households. Many old people are living separately from their children in Hong Kong. These social changes have caused substantial changes in household income distribution. Income Gini coefficient, claims Kwok, does not discern these structural changes in its society.<sup id="cite_ref-Kwok10_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kwok10-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Household money income distribution for the United States, summarized in Table C of this section, confirms that this issue is not limited to just Hong Kong. According to the US Census Bureau, between 1979 and 2010, the population of United States experienced structural changes in overall households, the income for all income brackets increased in inflation-adjusted terms, household income distributions shifted into higher income brackets over time, while the income Gini coefficient increased.<sup id="cite_ref-uscb2011_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uscb2011-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cbo.p.10_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cbo.p.10-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another limitation of Gini coefficient is that it is not a proper measure of <a href="/wiki/Egalitarianism" title="Egalitarianism">egalitarianism</a>, as it is only measures income dispersion. For example, if two equally egalitarian countries pursue different immigration policies, the country accepting a higher proportion of low-income or impoverished migrants will report a higher Gini coefficient and therefore may appear to exhibit more income inequality.\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Inability to value benefits and income from <a href="/wiki/Informal_sector" title="Informal sector">informal economy</a> affects Gini coefficient accuracy</dt></dl>\n<p>Some countries distribute benefits that are difficult to value. Countries that provide subsidized housing, medical care, education or other such services are difficult to value objectively, as it depends on quality and extent of the benefit. In absence of free markets, valuing these income transfers as household income is subjective. The theoretical model of Gini coefficient is limited to accepting correct or incorrect subjective assumptions.\n</p><p>In subsistence-driven and informal economies, people may have significant income in other forms than money, for example through <a href="/wiki/Subsistence_farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Subsistence farming">subsistence farming</a> or <a href="/wiki/Barter" title="Barter">bartering</a>. These income tend to accrue to the segment of population that is below-poverty line or very poor, in emerging and transitional economy countries such as those in sub-Saharan Africa, Latin America, Asia and Eastern Europe. Informal economy accounts for over half of global employment and as much as 90 per cent of employment in some of the poorer sub-Saharan countries with high official Gini inequality coefficients. Schneider et al., in their 2010 study of 162 countries,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> report about 31.2%, or about $20&#160;trillion, of world\'s <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a> is informal. In developing countries, the informal economy predominates for all income brackets except for the richer, urban upper income bracket populations. Even in developed economies, between 8% (United States) to 27% (Italy) of each nation\'s GDP is informal, and resulting informal income predominates as a livelihood activity for those in the lowest income brackets.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The value and distribution of the incomes from informal or underground economy is difficult to quantify, making true income Gini coefficients estimates difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-mfeld_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mfeld-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tandw_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tandw-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Different assumptions and quantifications of these incomes will yield different Gini coefficients.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gini has some mathematical limitations as well. It is not additive and different sets of people cannot be averaged to obtain the Gini coefficient of all the people in the sets.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives_to_Gini_coefficient">Alternatives to Gini coefficient</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gini_coefficient&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Alternatives to Gini coefficient">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Given the limitations of Gini coefficient, other statistical methods are used in combination or as an alternative measure of population dispersity. For example, <i>entropy measures</i> are frequently used (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Atkinson_index" title="Atkinson index">Atkinson index</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Theil_Index" class="mw-redirect" title="Theil Index">Theil Index</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mean_log_deviation" title="Mean log deviation">Mean log deviation</a> as special cases of the <a href="/wiki/Generalized_entropy_index" title="Generalized entropy index">generalized entropy index</a>). These measures attempt to compare the distribution of resources by intelligent agents in the market with a maximum <a href="/wiki/Information_entropy" class="mw-redirect" title="Information entropy">entropy</a> <a href="/wiki/Random_distribution" class="mw-redirect" title="Random distribution">random distribution</a>, which would occur if these agents acted like non-intelligent particles in a closed system following the laws of statistical physics.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_other_statistical_measures">Relation to other statistical measures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gini_coefficient&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Relation to other statistical measures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Gini coefficient is sometimes alternatively <i>defined</i> as twice the area between the <a href="/wiki/Receiver_operating_characteristic" title="Receiver operating characteristic">receiver operating characteristic</a> (ROC) curve and its diagonal, in which case the <a href="/wiki/Receiver_operating_characteristic#Area_under_curve" title="Receiver operating characteristic">AUC</a> (<a href="/wiki/Integral" title="Integral">Area Under</a> the ROC Curve) measure of performance is given by <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\\displaystyle AUC=(G+1)/2}">\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mi>A</mi>\n        <mi>U</mi>\n        <mi>C</mi>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mi>G</mi>\n        <mo>+</mo>\n        <mn>1</mn>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mo>/</mo>\n        </mrow>\n        <mn>2</mn>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle AUC=(G+1)/2}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e30686bb08576301cd2c30aade56036fc32ae2f9" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:18.354ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="AUC=(G+1)/2"/></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-hand_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hand-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>   The Gini coefficient is also closely related to <a href="/wiki/Mann%E2%80%93Whitney_U" class="mw-redirect" title="Mann\xe2\x80\x93Whitney U">Mann\xe2\x80\x93Whitney U</a>.\n</p><p>The Gini index is also related to Pietra index\xe2\x80\x94both of which are a measure of statistical heterogeneity and are derived from Lorenz curve and the diagonal line.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In certain fields such as ecology, inverse Simpson\'s index <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\\displaystyle 1/\\lambda }">\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mn>1</mn>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mo>/</mo>\n        </mrow>\n        <mi>&#x03BB;<!-- \xce\xbb --></mi>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle 1/\\lambda }</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1add931eabb9aec89275acb2f50d50428010b0fe" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:3.68ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="1/\\lambda "/></span> is used to quantify diversity, and this should not be confused with the <a href="/wiki/Diversity_index#Simpson_index" title="Diversity index">Simpson index</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\\displaystyle \\lambda }">\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mi>&#x03BB;<!-- \xce\xbb --></mi>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\lambda }</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b43d0ea3c9c025af1be9128e62a18fa74bedda2a" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.355ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="\\lambda "/></span>. These indicators are related to Gini. The inverse Simpson index increases with diversity, unlike Simpson index and Gini coefficient which decrease with diversity. The Simpson index is in the range [0, 1], where 0 means maximum and 1 means minimum diversity (or heterogeneity). Since diversity indices typically increase with increasing heterogeneity, Simpson index is often transformed into inverse Simpson, or using the complement <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\\displaystyle 1-\\lambda }">\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mn>1</mn>\n        <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n        <mi>&#x03BB;<!-- \xce\xbb --></mi>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle 1-\\lambda }</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/da4535bdb18a0914505c66a316f858f601be0cf8" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:5.358ex; height:2.343ex;" alt="{\\displaystyle 1-\\lambda }"/></span>, known as Gini-Simpson Index.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">Other uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gini_coefficient&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Although the Gini coefficient is most popular in economics, it can in theory be applied in any field of science that studies a distribution. For example, in ecology the Gini coefficient has been used as a measure of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>, where the cumulative proportion of species is plotted against cumulative proportion of individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-natureArticle_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natureArticle-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In health, it has been used as a measure of the inequality of health related <a href="/wiki/Quality_of_life" title="Quality of life">quality of life</a> in a population.<sup id="cite_ref-popHealthArticle_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popHealthArticle-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> In education, it has been used as a measure of the inequality of universities.<sup id="cite_ref-MinervaArticle_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MinervaArticle-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In chemistry it has been used to express the selectivity of <a href="/wiki/Protein_kinase_inhibitors" class="mw-redirect" title="Protein kinase inhibitors">protein kinase inhibitors</a> against a panel of kinases.<sup id="cite_ref-JMedChemArticle_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JMedChemArticle-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In engineering, it has been used to evaluate the fairness achieved by Internet routers in scheduling packet transmissions from different flows of traffic.<sup id="cite_ref-GreedyFairQueueing_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreedyFairQueueing-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Gini coefficient is sometimes used for the measurement of the discriminatory power of <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating" title="Credit rating">rating</a> systems in <a href="/wiki/Credit_risk" title="Credit risk">credit risk</a> management.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2005 study accessed US census data to measure home computer ownership, and used the Gini coefficient to measure inequalities amongst whites and African Americans. Results indicated that although decreasing overall, home computer ownership inequality is substantially smaller among white households.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2016 peer-reviewed study titled Employing the Gini coefficient to measure participation inequality in treatment-focused Digital Health Social Networks<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> illustrated that the Gini coefficient was helpful and accurate in measuring shifts in inequality, however as a standalone metric it failed to incorporate overall network size.\n</p><p>The discriminatory power refers to a credit risk model\'s ability to differentiate between defaulting and non-defaulting clients. The formula <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\\displaystyle G_{1}}">\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <msub>\n          <mi>G</mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mn>1</mn>\n          </mrow>\n        </msub>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle G_{1}}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e6ea4f4668b8334c8a7d3d284b0fd22131ef5f52" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.881ex; height:2.509ex;" alt="G_{1}"/></span>, in calculation section above, may be used for the final model and also at individual model factor level, to quantify the discriminatory power of individual factors. It is related to accuracy ratio in population assessment models.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gini_coefficient&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 22em; -webkit-column-width: 22em; column-width: 22em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Diversity_index" title="Diversity index">Diversity index</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">Economic inequality</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Great_Gatsby_curve" title="Great Gatsby curve">Great Gatsby curve</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Human_Poverty_Index" title="Human Poverty Index">Human Poverty Index</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Income_inequality_metrics" title="Income inequality metrics">Income inequality metrics</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kuznets_curve" title="Kuznets curve">Kuznets curve</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Pareto_distribution" title="Pareto distribution">Pareto distribution</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Hoover_index" title="Hoover index">Hoover index</a> (a.k.a. Robin Hood index)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/ROC_analysis" class="mw-redirect" title="ROC analysis">ROC analysis</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Social_welfare_provision" class="mw-redirect" title="Social welfare provision">Social welfare provision</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Suits_index" title="Suits index">Suits index</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">Utopia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Welfare_economics" title="Welfare economics">Welfare economics</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_distribution_of_wealth" title="List of countries by distribution of wealth">List of countries by distribution of wealth</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_income_equality" title="List of countries by income equality">List of countries by income equality</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_by_Gini_coefficient" title="List of U.S. states by Gini coefficient">List of U.S. states by Gini coefficient</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Herfindahl_index" title="Herfindahl index">Herfindahl index</a></li></ul></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gini_coefficient&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEGini1912-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGini1912_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFGini1912">Gini (1912)</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gini, C. United Nations Development Program. 2012.
